<div id="review_629711501" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/629711501" />
    <a title="Ash Wednesday" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10484016-ash-wednesday"><img alt="Ash Wednesday" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1379043440p2/10484016.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/629711501?book_show_action=true">May 31, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ash Wednesday" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ash Wednesday" href="/user/show/10484016-ash-wednesday">Ash Wednesday</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="ebook 9780545509909" href="/book/show/17071495-openly-straight">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Ash Wednesday by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/3973089-brigid">Brigid</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10484016-ash-wednesday?shelf=contemporary-romance">contemporary-romance</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10484016-ash-wednesday?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10484016-ash-wednesday?shelf=male-pov">male-pov</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10484016-ash-wednesday?shelf=humor">humor</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10484016-ash-wednesday?shelf=yaoi">yaoi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10484016-ash-wednesday?shelf=tbd-buddy-read">tbd-buddy-read</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer629711501" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9849293775137180347"><i>
  <b>4 STARS</b>
</i><br/><br/>There are books that will make you think, books that will make you laugh and a book that will change the way you listen to "Hollaback Girl".<br/><br/>But only one can give you all that… and moar.<br/><br/>I don't think I can write a review worthy of this book's wit and brilliance. I love how I never once stopped functioning as a thinking reader but it didn't cause me serious, debilitating pain in the process. No one got beaten up, no one nearly got raped, there was no crying in the rain while clutching s</span>
  <span id="freeText9849293775137180347" style="display:none"><i>
  <b>4 STARS</b>
</i><br><br>There are books that will make you think, books that will make you laugh and a book that will change the way you listen to "Hollaback Girl".<br><br>But only one can give you all that… and moar.<br><br>I don't think I can write a review worthy of this book's wit and brilliance. I love how I never once stopped functioning as a thinking reader but it didn't cause me serious, debilitating pain in the process. No one got beaten up, no one nearly got raped, there was no crying in the rain while clutching someone's soccer jersey. This was an absolute joy to experience. <br><br>It's tough to be Seamus Rafe Goldberg. To be openly gay in Boulder, Colorado since his freshman year; to be the only son of the president of Boulder PFLAG (Parents, Families and Friends of Lesbians and Gays); to have a best friend who calls you Shay Shay; to be a charming, complex and interesting young man boxed in a single word: GAY. Tough enough to leave this sheltered environment, pack his bags to an all-boys school in Natick with the singular purpose of reinventing himself to someone who's<b>
  <u> not just gay</u>
</b>. Not necessarily play the "Pretend Straight" game, just the "Maybe Not Mention" it to anyone. <br><br>Can you really extricate that part of yourself without losing who you are? <br><br>I have only read a handful of LGBT books but none of them satisfied what I was looking for as much as this one. Rafe's story is one of self-discovery, one that goes beyond his proclamation that he is gay. I liked that about this book. How a person is a person and not his sexual preference is a topic that should be heavy but the clever presentation of Rafe in this journey kept the drama to a minimum, the LOLs flowing freely and the wisdom in tofu pigs vividly painted. <br><br>Rafe should've really come across a whiner, a young man who cannot fully appreciate the gift of being gay in the 21st century. But his conflict and the questions he seeks answers from were fitting and valid for someone who is gay in the 21st century: when acceptance can easily translate to isolation, when someone's sexual orientation can be used as a weapon and when a guy just wants to be a guy who just happens to be gay.<br><br>The secondary characters were absolutely endearing. I covet Rafe's parents especially his mom (the gay guys gets the best moms, I think).<br><i>
  <b>
    <blockquote>"…Nothing that comes from love could ever be wrong."<br>It was just such a thing my mother would say. Then she started singing 'All You Need Is Love' and I excused myself because there's a certain level of cheese that's too goopy even for me.</blockquote>
  </b>
</i><br>I'm torn between Albie and Toby on who to trail in the event of a zombie apocalypse. Albie invented scanner pong but Toby dresses up like Michael Jackson and does a mean Oprah ("You get a car! You get a car!") so I guess I'll have to settle with Coach Donnelly who sounds like a cross between Ron Burgundy and Stephen Colbert.<br><br><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[
  <b>
    <i>
      <blockquote>"One day you have a father, and next, you're watching him fly a plane into a ship on Pearl Harbor on television. Those kids didn't do anything wrong. they just lived in an evil country. The axis of evil. That sort of evil is beyond anything you or I will experience in our lifetimes. So be glad. Be glad we live in the US of A. Be glad we get to choose, with our freedoms." </blockquote>
    </i>
  </b>
 <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380423670i/844228.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>If I have to gripe about some thing it would be that some of Rafe's motives were questionable. Did he really have to transfer to New England in an All-Boys' School to not be just "the gay guy"? Wouldn't that have worked as well in ANY school outside of Rawbridge? I actually liked how things ended, especially the chapter before the last. It was out of left field, very witty. But I'm not sure how I feel about <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ how things ended up between him and Ben. I'm not looking for a HEA between them, It just felt like Ben was collateral damage to Rafe's path to self-discovery. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> I'm not a fan of how and when <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[after sex with Ben, ofcourse <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> Rafe realized how he didn't like Steve who was depicted stereotypically as the testosterone-fueled jock. For a book with rich, multi-dimensional characters, Steve drew the shortest stick and got just one. I had to pause and think what didn't work for me in this one because it truly was a very well-written, entertaining story.<br><br>There's always going to be that one book for every genre that you'd auto-rec to anyone who asks. This is the one I'd recommend for anyone who wants to think and laugh while doing it. </["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></span>
  <a data-text-id="9849293775137180347" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1265895606" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1265895606" />
    <a title="Jason" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9104633-jason"><img alt="Jason" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1518977342p2/9104633.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1265895606?book_show_action=true">Apr 27, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jason" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jason" href="/user/show/9104633-jason">Jason</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1265895606" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer753495211671985816">Spoiler-ish note: the following discusses what happens to Rafe and Ben, but i think it's pretty clear that it becomes a cluster-fuck<br/><br/>As a middle schooler Rafe was out to his family, friends, and the entire community. His mother, stereotypically overbearing and insanely well adjusted to her son’s sexuality, Boudia-ed off her old life and into the life of a gay parent. Equally annoying—annoying because it stereotyped the type of man who accepts his son’s homosexuality—was his father, a man in-touch</span>
  <span id="freeText753495211671985816" style="display:none">Spoiler-ish note: the following discusses what happens to Rafe and Ben, but i think it's pretty clear that it becomes a cluster-fuck<br /><br />As a middle schooler Rafe was out to his family, friends, and the entire community. His mother, stereotypically overbearing and insanely well adjusted to her son’s sexuality, Boudia-ed off her old life and into the life of a gay parent. Equally annoying—annoying because it stereotyped the type of man who accepts his son’s homosexuality—was his father, a man in-touch with his feelings, overly zealous with recording movies on his cell phone, was a walking advertisement for a sedative. His best friend, Claire-Olivia (because we just needed an annoying name), is supportive, but also very much centered on her own life. There is a very stereotypical, feminine gay, because, well contrast. <br /><br />That was the life in Boulder, a life Raff left behind to attend a pre-school. He didn’t do this to advance his intellectual prowess. Oh no, he did it because he was fed up with his sexuality dictating his life. In Boulder people first and foremost identified him as gay, with all his other virtues and strengths secondary. He was a principle actor in the development of the community’s constricted vision of himself, and while Rafe fell to the pressure of his parents to out himself completely and participate in gay themed lectures, he was clearly adept at practicing self-agencies and self-determination. <br /><br />Secret Agent Straight was thrust into the world of an all male, and mostly straight, cohort, and was further imbedded into the jock scene. He assimilated quite well into this new role, and the novel doesn’t refrain from reinforcing his Secret Agent Straight status as being casual to his new affinity for sports, as well as his peers’ level of acceptance. Take home…. while in Boulder, gayness was a principle attribute in his exclusion from ‘being one of the guys’.<br /><br />Zoom forward with parties, drunkenness, awkwardness with a stereotypically weird and effeminate gay to contrast sharply with his otherwise Chameleon sexuality, dorks, lots of slut-shamming, various instances of homophobia and dangerously vapid discussions of it, some unexpected friends, and Secret Agent Straight all comfortable in his new factitious self. <br /><br />Pause in your zooming…… Secret Agent Straight find himself at the cusp of a budding male-bonding experience, the likes he never, according to him, would be able to have as a self identifying gay dude. Of course Ben, who sounds utterly adorable, yet a bit bottom heavy, was difficult to gauge earlier on in the book. I guess we needed that tension. But Ben loosens up as they romp among the unthinkable. Ben’s character develops clearer as the story progresses, and we find him to be a traitor to a childhood steeped in rigid conformity and all things republican. He is a lovable teddy bear and his interactions with Rafe were sooooo reminiscent of Ben and Tim in Something like Summer/Winter.<br /><br />There relationship blooms into something—if not tragically trite and expected. Both are left to navigate a situation that is uncomfortable. And duh, Secret Agent Straight feels incredibly guilty. Guilt swarms as he recognizes his actions and behavior have had a ripple effect on most, if not all of the systems of his life. <br /><br />I had my reservations when reading the blurb. I questioned whether this would be a successful look at a world flooded with heterosexual stereotypes and hypocrisies— metrosexual and bromance come to mine—, but I also wondered, ‘could and would this book become a vehicle for deconstructing, analyzing, and opening the door for dialog of all of the above?’. I was also fearful that it would sensationalize, that it would monopolize on some very challenging and contentious issues and that it would preach a position. Unfortunately at the conclusion of this book I realized that the latter was true. At face value this book offered a new and novel approach to the all to familiar gay coming of age book. However, peering deeper within the narrative and overall context revealed a few things that I found troubling. <br /><br />One of the clearest offenses was the loving and accepting family. Now, I realize these family units do exist (mine is), however I feel strongly that they engulf YA gay coming of age novels. I think this is a disservice to teenagers challenged by their sexual identify. It was further troubling to see the author construct a plot that had Rafe’s query of his own sexuality and the meaning of it, and all his support systems railing against Rafe. This is a huge double standard. At one point his family is ubber supportive, and then, when it doesn’t fit their specific needs or perceptions of sexuality, they fortify against him. Either way they are applying their lens to a very personal and individual thing. I was aghast. What does this tell young adults who are having a difficult time understanding their own sexuality and navigating the ways their family and friends may or are reacting to it? What does this reinforce for the child that has been ostracized for coming out? What does this tell a child who feels isolated and seeks out novels like this one for answers, only to find that maybe there aren’t people that will accept him if he doesn’t mend his sexual identity to their standards? <br /><br />Then we have another author subjecting his readers to the notion that being gay is an ugly, jagged collection of failures, fractured hopes, and a life of conformity (I’ll get into this sucker later!). Of course at the center of this novel is a relationship, what would a coming of age novel be without one? In this case we have Secret Agent Straight and Ben. Their relationship is inevitably hindered and restricted by Rafe’s hidden sexuality. Rafe find himself cornered by the life he created, unable to completely venture into a genuine intimate relationship. Yet, Rafe does find a semblance of one, and that is a hell of a lot better than forcing himself to endure sexual activity with people that neither deserved nor understood him. I question, again, what this tells a YA who is lonely and desperate for touch, intimacy, and a partner. What do you put up with? Being in a relationship that doesn’t fulfill you because you know you deserve more? Or being in a relationship that gives you some of the things you deserve, but because you are pretending to be someone you aren’t, you don’t get the whole apple pie? Hmmmm, which sounds worse? <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ This is made even worse cause he doesn’t find anyone.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>.<br /><br />Beyond the inescapable realization that Rafe’s dating life provided him something of substance when he was closeted—mind you an attribute that disgusted him when he was out—, we have that fucking concept that life will never be fulfilling, peaceful, and that the fear of being lonely or ostracized will never abate. Again, what the fuck are we telling teenagers when we illustrate this time and time again in books? <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ Like other novels, at the end, Secret Agent Straight was left regurgitate his past.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. Oh but wait, he learned something about himself from his relationship with Ben, like every other book with a similar climax. Yet, what exactly did he learn? Because it was so freaking illusive, that when splatter painted on the wall of other gay coming of age novels, and from affair, it is really difficult to see the subtle nuances.<br /><br />But, mostly, I felt saddened that it rejected its own premise. I assumed that it was attempting to decode the nature of labels, and that its final goal was to establish that one can have many different lenses. Unfortunately, once his life ruptured he was thrust back into his former existence. There were some philosophical discussions on the differences of self, specifically how he perceives himself in relation to others, but these were scant, and I felt rushed and under-developed. He left a life of a prominent label, being gay, and with it a life of stereotypes and forced ideology, and entered a life where he found love, comradery, and acceptance. Now, the argument will be that he ended up understanding that the foundation of those changes was false and he further felt that he betrayed himself, however, isn’t there a way he could have maintained some of these while also believing in himself and being happy? The problem is it ignores its own objective, that one can have multiple lenses and, through integrating them, be other than one’s sexuality. I get it, he learned that his former self wasn’t that bad, and in extracting some of Secret Agent Straight’s life, developed into this more self-enlightened individual, but I didn’t think this transformation varied from his original self, at least not by much. <br /><br />The scenario is troubling, specifically where stereotypes weren’t completely dismantled. Sports, an area that is plagued by homophobia, are the most prominent example. In Boulder Rafe felt anchored to a position of separate but equal. In MA, absent of the confines of his sexuality, he was easily accepted into this world. Bill really needs to have a sit down with Mr. Scarborough—I’d mock this more, but it was simply just a huge cop out to developing Rafe—because there is some serious meaning behind this message that requires some heavy probing. <br /><br />In the same vein there were additional troubling remarks made about masculinity. One of the larger was, “I hadn’t understood that desire in you, the desire to do those sort of boy things. I don’t know how I missed that”. My mouthed dropped as I felt the implications. It tied masculinity to being straight, and the exclusion of masculinity as an attribute of being gay. It also reinforced the deeply imbedded notion that some sports are gender specific. I felt that it also quite clearly implicated gays as girls, rather than boys. Likewise, with little discussion of the stereotypes that gays aren’t as able to participate in sports, it more than anything reinforced that Secret Agent Straight found the antidote to all these things; forfeiting one’s expression of his sexual identity. Ultimately it was a loss for an opportunity to explore these things in a way that was meaningful and constructive. <br /><br />We also had Rafe ignoring the all too familiar narrative that all homos are horny and want to fuck all straight people. Let me clear this up for you, we are, and we don’t. In not commenting when Steve said, “ I mean, we would have to figure out some other shower arrangement”, to which Rafe thinks, “I wanted to say: No, I don’t know. Not every guy wants to go to bed with you”, the author just really applied some sheet-rock to those perceptions. What a total fucking failure by the author. Lets not even talk about how it negates one’s ability to confront stereotypes.<br /><br />Overall it was well written, and the Ben and Rafe portions were so cuddly and adorable. It had some issues where it ignored sex, rather than exploring it tastefully. The addition of Mr. Scarborough caused pacing issues, seemed rather random, and was a cop-out to developing Rafe. But mostly I was left with a Rafe that was caught between embracing his sexuality in a manner he felt was his own and still relishing in the cold hard fact that Secret Agent Straight had it every so slightly good, minus having to jeopardize his integrity. This made me sad. <br /><br /><br /><br /><br />PS: why you gotta hate on a hairy butt, yo!</span>
  <a data-text-id="753495211671985816" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2355633214" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Atlas" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/38036841-atlas"><img alt="Atlas" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1438878473p2/38036841.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2355633214?book_show_action=true">Apr 09, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Atlas" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Atlas" href="/user/show/38036841-atlas">Atlas</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38036841-atlas?shelf=lgbt-characters">lgbt-characters</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2355633214" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10404938712864524866"><b>* * * * <br/>4 / 5<br/><br/>~mini review~<br/></b><br/>Openly Straight was emotional and angry and sad and such a fantastic read. Our main character Rafe has been out of the closet for a number of years and his parents have been massively supportive (perhaps overly so), but now it's gotten to the point where Rafe feels like being gay has become his sole defining quality. So when he transfers to an all-boys boarding school, Rafe decides that he isn't going to be as open about his sexuality as before. But then he meets Ben, q</span>
  <span id="freeText10404938712864524866" style="display:none"><b>* * * * <br />4 / 5<br /><br />~mini review~<br /></b><br />Openly Straight was emotional and angry and sad and such a fantastic read. Our main character Rafe has been out of the closet for a number of years and his parents have been massively supportive (perhaps overly so), but now it's gotten to the point where Rafe feels like being gay has become his sole defining quality. So when he transfers to an all-boys boarding school, Rafe decides that he isn't going to be as open about his sexuality as before. But then he meets Ben, questioning, uncertain, Ben. And it all falls apart.<br /><br />Rafe is a great main character. He's uncertain, irrational, sweet, angry, kind, and determined not to just be viewed as "the gay guy". But it seems like no one understands his desire - his mum thinks it's a personal attack that he wants to "retreat back into the closet", his best friend thinks he's lying to himself and others, and he's internally conflicted. Openly Straight really made me think, and it became clear that there really isn't any easy answers.<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://atlasrisingbooks.wordpress.com">http://atlasrisingbooks.wordpress.com</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="10404938712864524866" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2128128780" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2128128780" />
    <a title="Nina ✿ Looseleaf Reviews ✿" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3456885-nina-looseleaf-reviews"><img alt="Nina ✿ Looseleaf Reviews ✿" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1472096371p2/3456885.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2128128780?book_show_action=true">Sep 23, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nina ✿ Looseleaf Reviews ✿" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nina ✿ Looseleaf Reviews ✿" href="/user/show/3456885-nina-looseleaf-reviews">Nina ✿ Looseleaf Reviews ✿</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3456885-nina-looseleaf-reviews?shelf=e-books">e-books</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2128128780" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5045773687178426527">9/23/17<br/><br/>WOW. Like w o w. I goddamn loved this book! It's both a quirky high schoolers interacting feel-good kinda book and a gut wrenching exploration of identity and "coming out." I'm so glad I picked this up with the sequel already out, because there's no way I could wait to pick it up!<br/><br/>I have to sit on this review for a bit, but in the meantime, I'll leave this incoherent ramble to encourage everyone to read this asap. </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1956958945" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1956958945" />
    <a title="laurel [the suspected bibliophile]" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7494844-laurel-the-suspected-bibliophile"><img alt="laurel [the suspected bibliophile]" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1546974316p2/7494844.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1956958945?book_show_action=true">Jan 03, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="laurel [the suspected bibliophile]" class="user" itemprop="url" name="laurel [the suspected bibliophile]" href="/user/show/7494844-laurel-the-suspected-bibliophile">laurel [the suspected bibliophile]</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7494844-laurel-the-suspected-bibliophile?shelf=contemporary">contemporary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7494844-laurel-the-suspected-bibliophile?shelf=2019-read">2019-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7494844-laurel-the-suspected-bibliophile?shelf=lgbtq">lgbtq</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7494844-laurel-the-suspected-bibliophile?shelf=ya-books">ya-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7494844-laurel-the-suspected-bibliophile?shelf=audiobooks">audiobooks</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7494844-laurel-the-suspected-bibliophile?shelf=omfg-awful">omfg-awful</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7494844-laurel-the-suspected-bibliophile?shelf=ownvoices">ownvoices</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1956958945" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17167991049705260009"><b>Trigger Warning: heavy underage drinking, use of the f word (no, not fuck), a homophobic scene, drunken sex</b><br/><br/>Rafe is tired of being The Gay Kid at his school in Boulder, Colorado. He's tired of labels, of the constant eyes and judgement—even if he has a positive support structure, he wants a change of pace. And as a not gay, not straight, just Rafe student at a northeastern all-boys school, Rafe has his new start.<br/><br/>And then he meets Ben.<br/><br/>Cute and funny, there's just one problem: Ben is straight. But </span>
  <span id="freeText17167991049705260009" style="display:none"><b>Trigger Warning: heavy underage drinking, use of the f word (no, not fuck), a homophobic scene, drunken sex</b><br /><br />Rafe is tired of being The Gay Kid at his school in Boulder, Colorado. He's tired of labels, of the constant eyes and judgement—even if he has a positive support structure, he wants a change of pace. And as a not gay, not straight, just Rafe student at a northeastern all-boys school, Rafe has his new start.<br /><br />And then he meets Ben.<br /><br />Cute and funny, there's just one problem: Ben is straight. But Rafe thinks that that could change.<br />~<br />Hooooooooooooooooooooooo booooooooooooooooy.<br /><br />Based on the blurb, I was looking for an insightful, funny and empathetic story about two boys discovering a deep friendship and possibly exploration of sexuality between two boys in a hyper-masculine environment, with a learning curve character arc for Rafe as he realizes the impact of his lies, but not this mess of a book. <b>It had so much potential.</b><br /><br />Lemme just put the rest of this review into spoilers, because <b>spoilers are coming!</b><br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[<br /><b>Representation</b><br />For the subject matter, you'd think that there would be a little more representation and diversity in this book, particularly since Rafe was a member of PFLAG back in Colorado and spoke at a number of LGBT assemblies at schools and whatnot. But literally the only sexual identity on the LGBTQIA+ spectrum is gay men, with some commentary on bisexuality.<br /><br />Women are minimal, which is expected since the majority of the book is at an all-boys school, but Rafe's opinion of women is incredibly opinionated, with a really skeezy view of women and make-up. He goes on a little diatribe about how women wear make-up for their own vanity, and that it masks the "real" them, and that women in make-up are just <i>so</i> fake. This coming from a boy who is literally lying about being gay (when he was out of the closet before and had no issue with being out other than wanting to try something new)...and trying to sexualize his friendship with straight Ben.<br /><br />As for POC, as far as I could tell there was just one—Brice, Ben's former roommate. And Brice serves only as a plot device to get Ben and Rafe closer together before vanishing.<br /><br />As for the representation of experimenting with your sexuality...I liked Ben. I liked Ben a lot. He was smart, insightful and had the poor misfortune to catch Rafe's eye. (more on Rafe later) I really feel like the book should have been told from <i>his</i> point of view, and seeing as there is a companion novel that apparently came out that features him, perhaps there is. I will not be reading it.<br /><br />As for gay boy rep...hell naw. Rafe is an asshat who judges everyone else. He's particularly harshest on the other gay students—dissecting them by appearance, femininity, flamboyancy, mannerisms, everything. From a kid who has constant epiphanies about labels and projection of feelings, his vast monologues on the particular quirks and stereotypes of each boy is a little hypocritical.<br /><br /><b>Rafe</b><br />I tried to break this down a little more, but honestly this is such an introspective book that gets so deep (or does it?) into the mind of the MC that it's hard to separate further.<br /><br />But, to begin: Rafe is a hypocritical, manipulating, little asshat.<br /><br />My evidence:<br /><br />1. Upper middle class, white cis gay boy wants to shed his label of being "gay," and so pretends to be straight at his new swanky school. He leaves without much thought for his best friend, and arrives with the protestations that he's not in the closet, <i>oh no</i>, he's just not declaring one way or the other—however, despite his idea that if he's asked outright he'll tell, he gets defensive and angry when people ask him. My boy—<i>that is still in the closet</i>. Also, being able to shed a label and kinda pass as straight is the very essence of privilege. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[I fucking say this as a bi white woman married to a lesbian. I pass as straight. I'm white. It's a fucking privilege. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br /><i>Guys: It's okay if you're ga—<br />Rafe: wth makes you think I'm gay?<br />Guys: Um, well you're a little different and that's oka—<br />Rafe: How dare you try to put a label on me? I don’t want labels. I’m just Rafe! Label free Rafe! God guys!<br />Rafe: *proceeds to label and judge everyone around him*</i><br /><br />2. <b>Rafe's relationship with Ben is all sorts of twisted.</b> Rafe has a crush on Ben. He works his way into a friendship, insinuating himself ever further into every aspect of Ben's life. He becomes Ben's confidante, not just with the idea that he wants to be best friends, but because he wants Ben to be his boyfriend. Rafe's ulterior motive—and his refusal to tell Ben about his orientation despite all of his hand-wringing about it—along with his refusal to really listen to Ben and turn all conversations about anything to himself (Rafe) mitigates any sympathy I might have for him. <br /><br /><i>Ben: I’m straight<br />Rafe: well what if<br />Ben: naw, my feelings are pretty clear<br />Rafe: what about...?<br />Ben: dude if i was gay id love you like that but i don-<br />Rafe: *kisses Ben on the mouth*</i><br /><br /><i>guys this was literally a scene in this blasted book</i> Which brings me to my next point.<br /><br />3. <b>Consent</b>. Despite Ben's <i>many</i> protestations to the contrary, Rafe continues to push. If they were truly two boys experimenting with their sexuality, this could be chocked up to two curious people trying something new. One might be reluctant, but still curious. And yet. (see 4).<br /><br />Also, that sex scene. HOoooooooooOOOLy shiiIIIIIIiiite. <b>Ben is intoxicated</b>. Rafe is not. Rafe comes to Ben's room. He kisses Ben, tastes the alcohol on his mouth, and then convinces him to have sex with him. <br /><br />This is not a tender scene of finding out your sexuality. This is icky as fuck.<br /><br />4. As to the experimenting thing. <b>Literally Ben's entire argument with Rafe once Rafe's sexuality is revealed is that Ben is upset because he feels betrayed.</b><br /><br /><b>This lie goes beyond a lie.</b><br /><br />Ben believed (and he says this) that he and Rafe were on equal standing. That they were two boys who believed they were straight, who felt such a <b>deep</b> connection of love and trust to each other that they wanted to explore that feeling. Maybe it made them gay. Maybe it didn't. But they were venturing into that journey <i>together</i>. They were taking that roller coaster ride for the first time together.<br /><br />Except Rafe had already ridden that roller coaster before. And he hid it from Ben. Perhaps he didn't want his sexuality to get in the way of their relationship, but he very much wanted them to be boyfriends <b>so much so</b> that he didn't tell Ben about his sexuality or his previous partner.<br /><br />Rafe had fucking skied down a double black diamond while Ben hadn't ever put on skis.<br /><br />5. The fact that Number 4 is completely, utterly and absolutely unmentioned during Rafe's epiphany is utterly reprehensible. Rafe's <i>epiphany</i> is that he hurt Ben because he lied about his sexuality, that he didn't share a key part of his personality.<br /><br />Not once does he ever think about Ben's complaint that they were on two different levels of experience. About how utterly that betrayal must have been to Ben, that feeling of being manipulated and used.<br /><br />It probably didn't help that every time Ben brought up his feelings or his anger, Rafe turned it back on Ben and made it about Rafe and his issues. Ben kept saying "I <i>can't be gay</i>" and instead of exploring and understanding, Rafe gaslighted him.<br /><br />Moving on.<br /><br />6.<b>Rafe's hypocritical judgment.</b> Mentioned earlier, but while Rafe moans and whines about being <i>labeled</i>, he constantly judges, criticizes and labels literally everyone else. There's a brief moment of insight when he's talking with fellow students of the GSA, but then he quickly reverts back into judging and thinking about how others might be looking at him.<br /><br />7. He's a narcissistic, self-centered asshat (granted, 95% of teen boys so this is a pass) who forces every single argument back into his woes or turning himself into the victim. And this does not change throughout the book. For all his grand insights, no personal growth is really made.<br /><br />8. <b>He is an unreliable narrator.</b> Why? Because most of this book is journal entries written to his creative writing teacher. Not written <i>for</i> the assignment, but written to someone. As Rafe himself puts it, he's constantly pretending to be performing on a stage.<br /><br />Despite his "honesty," there's always the sense that he's trying to put himself into the best light—particularly when he tries to rationalize his horrible actions or side-step the majority of responsibility for his decision. He makes apples and oranges comparisons the mitigate the impact of his own mistakes.<br /><br /><i>And you can't trust a damn thing he says.</i><br /><br />Case in point: his "Hi Mr S, it's really me talking! This is the <i>real</i> Rafe! I now get what you've been trying to tell me all along" really rings false, particularly since his voice and thoughts change not a bit.<br /><br />9. And on top of all of that, Rafe's parents and his best girl who is a friend tell him that he's really a great guy who made one tiny mistake and owned up for it. No harm, no foul.<br /><br />Even when Ben calls him out on his crap and Rafe cries to everyone else about it, they tell him that it's not him. He's fine. Ben's just upset and will be okay. And Rafe has owned up to his lie and is a great person who did a bag thing. But he's still great.<br /><br /><b>ARE YOU FUCKING KIDDING ME?!</b> <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />Okay, so clearly I have ~feelings~ about this book that make this review about as far from objective as possible.<br /><br />I even made notes while listening to the audiobook, and I hardly ever take notes while reading a book unless I'm doing research.<br /><br />Two stars because <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ the <b>one</b> good thing about this book? Ben says FUCK YOU and they don't get together, even if Rafe thinks there's potential for it later down the road <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span></span>
  <a data-text-id="17167991049705260009" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1774798203" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15276572990516882163"><blockquote>
  <i>
    <b>I created a barrier getting rid of a barrier.</b>
  </i>
</blockquote><br/><i>Openly Straight</i> is primarily an important book.<br/>It’s also a pretty enjoyable read, and it can be read as just that, but I think where it shines is its ability to make you think about the way we label ourselves and others.<br/>Does it give you a definite answer? No. If you’re looking for a book that will hand you an easy, universal truth, this book is not it. <br/><br/>But let’s talk about the content first.<br/><br/>Rafe is out of the closet, and in his hometown ev</span>
  <span id="freeText15276572990516882163" style="display:none"><blockquote>
  <i>
    <b>I created a barrier getting rid of a barrier.</b>
  </i>
</blockquote><br /><i>Openly Straight</i> is primarily an important book.<br />It’s also a pretty enjoyable read, and it can be read as just that, but I think where it shines is its ability to make you think about the way we label ourselves and others.<br />Does it give you a definite answer? No. If you’re looking for a book that will hand you an easy, universal truth, this book is not it. <br /><br />But let’s talk about the content first.<br /><br />Rafe is out of the closet, and in his hometown everyone is pretty cool with it, his parents most of all. They go as far as throwing him coming out parties and whatever, and Rafe was both aware that this is much better than being rejected by his family, like many LGBT+ teens are, and fed up with the way they handled the whole thing.<br /><blockquote>
  <i>“I know, I’m gay. I’m your gay son. But could you just give me a fucking break for two minutes so I can be just me too?”</i>
</blockquote><br />When a label becomes all you are in the eyes of the world, that’s pretty tough, whether you are in a welcoming environment or not. Especially if you’re still a teen and you’re trying to figure out <i>who</i> you are, like all the teens in the world. That’s not easy, ever, and it doesn’t help to see everybody remind you that “you’re the gay guy”. <br />Like when a reporter comes at you after a soccer game with a microphone in your face and all he says is, <i>“The gay guy won the game!”</i> Like that has anything to do with the way you kick a ball.<br /><br />That’s why Rafe decided to switch high school and not come out as gay there, effectively returning to the closet.<br />Without the <i>barrier</i> (his words, not mine) of the gay-label, he feels like he can enjoy his male friendships in a way that he wasn’t able to do before.<br />Soon enough, he finds true friendship in Ben.<br /><blockquote>
  <i>“I guess I’d like to think of what we have as <b>agape</b>. A higher love. Something that transcends. Something not about sex or brotherhood but about two people truly connecting.”</i>
</blockquote><br />But that’s when things start to backfire, because not coming out becomes lying, and not just a simple omission.<br /><br />This brings me back to what I said in the beginning. <br /><br /><i>Openly Straight</i> gives you two sides to reflect on: a kid who wants to “go back to the closet” and a family and friends that call him out because of it.<br />Granted that being in or out is a personal decision, I feel like I could, in a way, relate to his family as well when they didn’t agree with his decision. However, I also really despised his family for the way they called him out, essentially making it about themselves most of the time, just the way they made it about themselves when he first came out to them.<br />While this behavior is perhaps not clearly called out right away, I could feel Rafe’s annoyance fuse with mine while I was reading these scenes, but I also found it good that he didn’t completely shut them out of his life.<br />As you can see, it’s not that I’m conflicted about what side to be on, it’s more than the author did a good job at letting me see the positives and negatives of both sides, because this is not a black and white issue (like many things in life).<br />Some people might argue that this is a YA work destined to teens that might want a straight-up answer, but I think teens and young adults are clever enough to be able to discern positive and negative behaviors by themselves. This book gives you some food for thought and I think that’s one of the best way to make you an aware adult who can, you know, <i>think</i>.<br /><br />Speaking of the <b>characters</b>, they were all pretty great (except the problems I have with Rafe’s family). <br />The best ones besides Rafe himself (who is a normal, imperfect character) were Mr. Scarborough and Ben.<br /><blockquote>
  <i>“It’s hard to be different,” Scarborough said. “And perhaps the best answer is not to tolerate differences, not even to accept them. But to celebrate them. Maybe then those who are different would feel more loved, and less, well, tolerated.”</i>
</blockquote><br />Listen, my mom is a teacher, my grandpa was a teacher, I <b>hate</b> it when teachers are only portrayed as evil beings. Of course those exist as well (I’ve had too many in school and uni), but there so many awesome teachers as well and I melt whenever there’s a positive representation of them in a YA book.<br /><br />Rafe’s other friends, Toby and Albie, were pretty amazing as well, and I loved that Rafe didn’t ignore them or make fun of them for being a bit “weird” even when he was in the jocks group. I loooove positive friendships and this book excelled at them.<br /><br />The sequel is coming out soon (tomorrow!) and it will be Ben’s PoV and I can’t wait to read it.<br /><br />Overall this was a pretty solid <b>4,5 stars</b> (rounded down because I don't feel like it can compete with other 5-stars favorites of mine)</span>
  <a data-text-id="15276572990516882163" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer152768975281471475">Absolutely awful.<br/><br/>Rafe as a person is impossible to relate to, despite how easy it should be. But a gay kid who feels so accepted he has to move to an all boys' school to pretend to be straight? Sounds like a poorly written fanfiction. (And yes, I am aware that Rafe felt he was just becoming "that gay kid" but he realizes at the end this wasn't true.   <span id="freeText152768975281471475" style="display:none">Absolutely awful.<br /><br />Rafe as a person is impossible to relate to, despite how easy it should be. But a gay kid who feels so accepted he has to move to an all boys' school to pretend to be straight? Sounds like a poorly written fanfiction. (And yes, I am aware that Rafe felt he was just becoming "that gay kid" but he realizes at the end this wasn't true. So the point of this all was???)<br /><br />Rafe is a detestable human being, made even worse by the fact that we're supposed to like him. His relationships with others are despicable (everything with Ben was horrid, and Ben deserves so much better) and his humor is dry.<br /><br />PS,<br /><br />"It was like when you approach a woman whom you think is beautiful and you see the caked-on blush and mascara, and you realize what you are seeing isn't her; it's her vanity. Konigsberg, women get enough of this horse manure from straight men who think they know what's best. People who wear makeup don't need it from you, too. Makeup is not a mask. It is not vanity. It is not a false face. People do not wear makeup because they don't want you to know what they really look like. Makeup is not the opposite of beauty or the attempt at beauty. It is makeup. It's been around for a long time. It's not evil. And people who chose to wear it do not need or deserve your or your character's scorn. It's a disgusting concept, that people who wear makeup are somehow hiding themselves or trying to achieve something they don't have.</span>

<div id="review_1753797800" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1753797800" />
    <a title="Elle (ellexamines)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/52545435-elle-ellexamines"><img alt="Elle (ellexamines)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1582250895p2/52545435.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1753797800?book_show_action=true">Sep 09, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Elle (ellexamines)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Elle (ellexamines)" href="/user/show/52545435-elle-ellexamines">Elle (ellexamines)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/52545435-elle-ellexamines?shelf=z-read2015">z-read2015</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/52545435-elle-ellexamines?shelf=lgbtq">lgbtq</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/52545435-elle-ellexamines?shelf=contemporary">contemporary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/52545435-elle-ellexamines?shelf=what-is-this-mess">what-is-this-mess</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/52545435-elle-ellexamines?shelf=3-star">3-star</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1753797800" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12939279509818171271">3.5 stars. Very mixed feelings; this book and the second book don’t form a coherent enough narrative, yet the romance plot and characters are certainly worthwhile. <br/><br/>In terms of character, this book absolutely suceeds. Rafe has a realistic, believable voice, and isn’t too perfect while also not being an asshole. Ben is a sweet guy while also not being a manic pixie dream boy. Their relationship is built up slowly in a great way. I hated the ending, but I understand why it was written. (Yay for the</span>
  <span id="freeText12939279509818171271" style="display:none">3.5 stars. Very mixed feelings; this book and the second book don’t form a coherent enough narrative, yet the romance plot and characters are certainly worthwhile. <br /><br />In terms of character, this book absolutely suceeds. Rafe has a realistic, believable voice, and isn’t too perfect while also not being an asshole. Ben is a sweet guy while also not being a manic pixie dream boy. Their relationship is built up slowly in a great way. I hated the ending, but I understand why it was written. (Yay for their ending in the sequel!)<br /><br /><i>Openly Straight</i> has some great exploration of labels and what they mean. <br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i>“We’re a pretty tolerant place,” Steve said, an edge to his voice.<br />“Ah, interesting word. Tolerant. What does tolerant mean?”<br />“It means we tolerate,” Steve said, flat. “We accept people.” <br />“Actually, tolerance and acceptance are different. To tolerate seems to mean that there is something negative to tolerate, doesn't it? Acceptance, though, what's that?”<br />“Well, if you need to accept something, that means it's not like it should be, right? Like you accept something as it is. Acceptance is an affirmation you're good enough.”</i>
</blockquote><br /><br />Quotes like this landed perfectly. I also loved that Konigsberg never acted as if he had all the answers about sexuality and heteronormativity. However, this aspect fails in several ways. <br /><br />First of all, Rafe’s decision to closet himself is treated as a personal offense by both his best friend and his parents, and they are all presented as correct in their anger at Rafe. While he’s definitely denying himself, his closest friends all act as if he is personally attacking them by being closeted. That’s not a good message. <b>Being in or out of the closet is a personal decision.</b> Rafe wanted to make friends with dudebro guys without being judged. That’s an understandable decision; even many purportedly non-homophobic people really do treat gay people differently, conciously or not. You have no idea how many of my friends started cringing at hugs after I came out. Rafe’s motivations were understandable! He just wanted to be thought of as himself, rather than as “that gay dude.” I do want to applaud the realness of this narrative; unfortunately, the “lessons” <i>Openly Straight</i> teaches us about that desire aren’t exactly flawless. <br /><br />The narrative of the book seems to revolve more around whether Rafe is "labeling himself” rather than closeting himself. It’s confusing and odd, because Rafe isn’t confused about his sexuality; he’s just in the closet for safety reasons. Again, being in the closet is a personal decision, and the way the narrative handled it… wasn’t perfect. <br /><br />I also disliked the resolution of the soccer sideplot. The ending essentially implies that gay guys actually can't be friends with the popular jocks. Why couldn't at least some of the soccer guys have been cool? <br /><br />The book also uses the word “retard” without calling it out, which strikes me as kind of terrible for being written in 2013. There’s also the quote “It was like when you approach a woman whom you think is beautiful and you see the caked-on blush and mascara, and you realize what you are seeing isn’t her; it’s her vanity.” That’s… um, a little sexist. Yes, these are nitpicks, but seriously, way to make me cringe. <br /><br />I do recommend this duology, despite my mixed feelings, if only for the strength of the characters.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12939279509818171271" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_626697015" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/626697015" />
    <a title="Kaje Harper" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5309738-kaje-harper"><img alt="Kaje Harper" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1307630447p2/5309738.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/626697015?book_show_action=true">May 28, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kaje Harper" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kaje Harper" href="/user/show/5309738-kaje-harper">Kaje Harper</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5309738-kaje-harper?shelf=ya">ya</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5309738-kaje-harper?shelf=m-m">m-m</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer626697015" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1144630531126827081">The main character in this book does something we all sometimes dream about - he reinvents himself. Moving across the country and starting boarding school gives Rafe the chance to try out who he would be, if he hadn't come out as gay to everyone he knows back in middle school. He wants to see what life is like in eleventh grade if the guys around him don't look at him and see "the gay kid". He doesn't consider it being in the closet, exactly, since he isn't planning to lie. Except perhaps by mis</span>
  <span id="freeText1144630531126827081" style="display:none">The main character in this book does something we all sometimes dream about - he reinvents himself. Moving across the country and starting boarding school gives Rafe the chance to try out who he would be, if he hadn't come out as gay to everyone he knows back in middle school. He wants to see what life is like in eleventh grade if the guys around him don't look at him and see "the gay kid". He doesn't consider it being in the closet, exactly, since he isn't planning to lie. Except perhaps by misdirection and omission. But he wants to fly under the radar and see what leaving that label behind is like.<br /><br />What he discovers is that it's never that simple. He gets to hang out with the straight boys and be one of the gang. But that means sometimes going against his own instincts. He does have to come close to, right up to, okay over the line of, lying, to keep up his straight facade. And he is still gay underneath, which means that when he starts falling for someone it's another boy. Who is straight. Probably.<br /><br />This is an interesting look at what it means to wear labels, and how much we owe it to ourselves and to others to expose ourselves in the name of being true to who we are. How much can we hide and still be enough ourselves to have an honest relationship? When is it wrong not to let someone in? And how do we handle it when people's perceptions of us change with our self-revelation.<br /><br />This was a slow, interesting, coming-of-age story. Well written, although the teacher-comments on the little flash-back journal entries were more annoying than enlightening to me. The secondary characters were fun, if perhaps a bit over the top at times. This book will make you think, even if the resolution isn't where you want it to be. <br /><br />This is YA, where personal growth is the endpoint. I really wanted a couple more chapters, to find out what happened when Rafe went back to school. But maybe there will be a sequel. And it did make me spend time wondering about when and how we decide to tell people difficult truths. How much can you hide and not lose integrity? No one knows all of us, ever, so what parts of our inner selves need to be shared when? I'm not sure my conclusions are entirely the same as the author's but I was grateful for the journey.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1144630531126827081" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('oqv0F/HL8yxdUlfc/Yhjkk0b+GIpN0n6RZS7wcMYqdpaHVXgLJYVkCDN6fHgyR0XwUy2TUgFT3FCSwVAvgQN8A==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  }

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/1304838.Bill_Konigsberg">About Bill Konigsberg</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer8071366505447657008">Bill Konigsberg was born in 1970 in New York City. Expectations were high from birth - at least in terms of athletics. His parents figured he'd be a great soccer player, based on his spirited kicking from inside the womb. As it turned out, the highlight of his soccer career was at Camp Greylock in 1978, when he was chosen for the Camp's "D" team. There were only four levels. Bill played alongside </span>
  <span id="freeText8071366505447657008" style="display:none">Bill Konigsberg was born in 1970 in New York City. Expectations were high from birth - at least in terms of athletics. His parents figured he'd be a great soccer player, based on his spirited kicking from inside the womb. As it turned out, the highlight of his soccer career was at Camp Greylock in 1978, when he was chosen for the Camp's "D" team. There were only four levels. Bill played alongside the likes of the kid who always showered alone, the chronic nosebleeder and the guy with recurrent poison ivy.<br /><br />Early in his life, Bill decided he wanted to be a disc jockey, a professional baseball player, or the Construction Worker from The Village People. None of these career paths worked out for him. Yet. He still holds out hope for a Village People revival and has set up a Google Alert in case it happens.<br /><br />A B- student throughout high school, Bill was voted Most Likely to Avoid Doing Any Real Work In His Life by a panel of his dismissive peers. He proved them wrong with a series of strange-but-true jobs in his 20s - driver recruiter for a truck driving school, sales consultant for a phone company, and temp at Otis Elevators.<br /><br />He moved to Denver in 1996 and was voted Least Stylish Gay Guy in the Metro Denver Area (including Loveland!) for each of the years from 1996-98. His fashion-free wardrobe robbed him of prospective dates countless times, as did his penchant for wearing a mustache that didn't suit him.<br /><br />He worked at ESPN and ESPN.com from 1999-2002, where he developed a penchant for sharing too much information about himself. That character flaw earned him a GLAAD Media Award in 2002, for his column "Sports World Still a Struggle for Gays." That coming out essay made him a household name to tens of people across the country.<br /><br />He continued oversharing in graduate school at Arizona State, where he added People Pleasing to his growing list of character defects and parlayed that into the title of Most Chill Teacher of freshman composition.<br /><br />As a sports writer and editor for The Associated Press in New York from 2005-08, Bill once called his husband, who was at the time working a desk job, from the New York Mets dugout before a game. "I'm so bored," Bill whined. He slept on the couch for a week after making that call.<br /><br />He wrote a novel called Audibles at Arizona State, and sold that novel to Dutton Books for Children in 2007. His editor asked him to change the title so that it would appeal to people other than "football players who read." The resulting novel, Out of the Pocket, received strong reviews from his mother, father, significant other and one girl who had a crush on him in high school. It won the Lambda Literary Award in 2009.<br /><br />His second novel, Openly Straight, hit the bookshelves in late May of 2013. He describes the novel as "Twilight-like, only without vampires and wolves and angsty teenage girls. Also, set in an all-boys boarding school in Massachusetts. Otherwise, it's like an exact replica."<br /><br />His third novel, The Porcupine of Truth, was released in May of 2015. He chose to put a porcupine in the title because this is America, and no one here knows what a platypus is. The novel won the Stonewall Book Award and PEN Center USA Literary Award.<br /><br />Next came Honestly Ben, a companion book to Openly Straight. He wrote it so people would stop yelling at him about Openly Straight's ending. Honestly Ben received three starred reviews and made lots of people swoon over Ben some more, which irks Bill to no end as Ben is loosely based on his husband, Chuck. No one seems to swoon over Rafe, who is loosely based on Bill. Harrumph, says Bill.<br /><br />The Music of What Happens arrives in February of 2019, and it's a romance between two boys, and it includes a food truck that makes cloud eggs. Bill has an egg phobia.<br /><br />Bill currently lives in Chandler, Arizona, which is the thinking man's Gilbert, Arizona. He has a husband who is clearly too good for him, and two cute dogs, Mabel and Buford, who complete him.<br /><br />His bl</span>
